  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_2cf6b4fc-4f7e-4436-afd8-85d9a8ed870c'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1682276691" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16369990715075989270"><b>
  <u>Endless entertainment!</u>
</b><br/><br/><br/><i>This volumen is a special presenting short stories featuring each member of The Endless.</i><br/><br/><br/><i>The general rating is an average sum of the ratings given to each story contained in this comic book.</i><br/><br/><br/>All stories are written by Neil Gaiman.<br/><br/>A very amusing thing is that while each story is focused in a member of The Endless (the family of Morpheus, the Lord of Dream), you may be able to appreciate how each other member of The Endless causes some level of impact in some way or another</span>
  <span id="freeText16369990715075989270" style="display:none"><b>
  <u>Endless entertainment!</u>
</b><br /><br /><br /><i>This volumen is a special presenting short stories featuring each member of The Endless.</i><br /><br /><br /><i>The general rating is an average sum of the ratings given to each story contained in this comic book.</i><br /><br /><br />All stories are written by Neil Gaiman.<br /><br />A very amusing thing is that while each story is focused in a member of The Endless (the family of Morpheus, the Lord of Dream), you may be able to appreciate how each other member of The Endless causes some level of impact in some way or another, and without a doubt while The Endless are the protagonist of their short stories, you can’t deny the importance of people. Without people, The Endless are nothing, they couldn’t even exist.<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>DEATH AND VENICE</u>
</b><br /><br />Rating: **** ( 4 stars )<br /><br />Illustrator: P. Craig Russell<br /><br /><b>
  <i>I missed you… so badly.</i>
</b><br /><br />In this story, Death is waiting for a door to be open again and getting throught it, a very special door.<br /><br />A man who has been blessed with watching Death (when it’s not still his time) will be key to this.<br /><br />An interesting tale about various lives when they encounter a perfect day.<br /><br />How tempting is to live always in a perfect day?<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>WHAT I’VE TASTED OF DESIRE</u>
</b><br /><br />Rating: ***** ( 5 stars )<br /><br />Illustrator: Milo Manara<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Follow your heart.</i>
</b><br /><br />A woman wants to be loved by someone in the same way that she’d love that someone too.<br /><br />Ooooh, that’s good that she didn’t want anything hard! Geez!<br /><br />Desire meets that woman and he/she shows to the woman her inner nature. That helped her to define her path in life.<br /><br />Is it possible to have anything you want in life?<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>DREAM THE HEART OF A STAR</u>
</b><br /><br />Rating: ***** ( 5 stars )<br /><br />Illustrator: Miguelanxo Prado<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Just because a name is used by the young doesn’t make it foolish.</i>
</b><br /><br />Easily the best story in the bunch.<br /><br />Morpheus invites his girlfriend, Killalla of the Glow, to a very important parliament where the high powers will discuss and delimitate their own reaches, domains, responsibilities and obligations.<br /><br />All the Endless are invited, along with many other high powers, like Stars, Faerie, Dimensions. <br /><br />Organic life is quite at minimum in the universe. Most planets are asleep yet.<br /><br />Dream and Desire are still close to each other, but you will find out the reason of their distancing.<br /><br />Delight is still Delight, not yet Delirium.<br /><br />Destruction is still in his post.<br /><br />Despair, mmh… Despair is there too.<br /><br />Destiny and Death will do brief appearances.<br /><br />You will have a real treat meeting the stars of Rao, Sto-Oa, Sol, etc…<br /><br />How to refuse such priceless invitation?<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>FIFTEEN PORTRAITS OF DESPAIR</u>
</b><br /><br />Rating: ** ( 2 stars )<br /><br />Illustrator: Barron Storey (with such last name, he shouldn’t be a writer?)<br /><br />Original design: Dave McKean<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Her sigil is the hooked ring.</i>
</b><br /><br />A very bizarre graphic narrative that I guess its intention is to provoke a sense of despair.<br /><br />Well, what else could you expect from this Endless member?<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>DELIRIUM GOING INSIDE</u>
</b><br /><br />Rating: ** ( 2 stars )<br /><br />Illustrator: Bill Sienkiewicz<br /><br /><b>
  <i>She’s still inside.</i>
</b><br /><br />A very delirious tale about pain and how to deal with it.<br /><br />Certainly, you hadn’t expected anything coherent from her, mmh?<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>DESTRUCTION</u>
</b><br /><br />Rating: **** ( 4 stars )<br /><br />Illustrator: Glenn Fabry<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Hey. Have you had any experience? With ruins?</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>
  <i>I’ve certainly made my share of them.</i>
</b><br /><br />A female arqueologist is invited to investigate a strange mountain, a mountain that it wasn’t there just a year ago, and where you find impossible things while digging.<br /><br />Destruction already left his post, but he is still around and he finds to develop an interest on the arqueologist.<br /><br />How Destruction can resist a woman who makes a living on digging destroyed things?<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <u>DESTINY ENDLESS NIGHTS</u>
</b><br /><br />Rating: **** ( 4 stars )<br /><br />Illustrator: Frank Quitely<br /><br /><b>
  <i>He did not create the path you walk.</i>
</b><br /><br />A simple but effective tale explaining the role and the burden of the oldest member of The Endless.<br /><br />How could you resist to take a peek in a book where you could know everything that happened but also everything that will happen about you or anybody else? <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16369990715075989270" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=4-star-reads">4-star-reads</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2349254419" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13336766389496402278">Seven siblings that are each more powerful than gods in their own way make for one rather odd and insane family. Who wouldn’t want to be part of it? <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524752138i/25437135._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='300' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1522780965i/25318210._SX540_.jpg' width='400' height='300' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I just love the concept behind it all. In here they all have their own issue to establish exactly what their purposes within the universe are. In a way, they all work together to create much of reality. Without Destiny there could be no dreams or desires. Without dreams there could be no delirium or despair. And without destruction there would be no</span>
  <span id="freeText13336766389496402278" style="display:none">Seven siblings that are each more powerful than gods in their own way make for one rather odd and insane family. Who wouldn’t want to be part of it? <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1524752138i/25437135._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1522780965i/25318210._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I just love the concept behind it all. In here they all have their own issue to establish exactly what their purposes within the universe are. In a way, they all work together to create much of reality. Without Destiny there could be no dreams or desires. Without dreams there could be no delirium or despair. And without destruction there would be no moving force in which destiny could act or no end for death to claim. Take one element out, and the others are not quite as effective.<br><br>As much as I liked seeing their own stories, I much prefer seeing them in the same room. They all have volatile personalities and some are contrasted and diametrically opposed. They come together rarely and very rarely altogether. When they do though very interesting things happen, I would have like to see more of that here.<br><br>For me this is a good introduction to the characters, which I want to see expanded on.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11681122972542929190">Re-read 9/30/20:<br/><br/>Between the *mostly* gorgeous artwork (Despair aside -- there's nothing in Despair I actually ENJOYED reading) and the poignant tales of each of the Endless, it turns out I'm still a fan.<br/><br/><br/>Original Review:<br/><br/>This one is more in line with the rest of the series, disjointed but interwoven with stories, odd artwork and sometimes painful text. In other words, part of the classic series.<br/><br/>With one caveat.<br/><br/>I loved it to death for a slightly different reason than the rest. They were individual </span>
  <span id="freeText11681122972542929190" style="display:none">Re-read 9/30/20:<br /><br />Between the *mostly* gorgeous artwork (Despair aside -- there's nothing in Despair I actually ENJOYED reading) and the poignant tales of each of the Endless, it turns out I'm still a fan.<br /><br /><br />Original Review:<br /><br />This one is more in line with the rest of the series, disjointed but interwoven with stories, odd artwork and sometimes painful text. In other words, part of the classic series.<br /><br />With one caveat.<br /><br />I loved it to death for a slightly different reason than the rest. They were individual stories of each of the Endless, both deep into their lives and bursting with history and it was all damn interesting. I didn't like Despair's nearly as much as I wanted to, but Death and the very young Dream and especially Desire's stories were awesome.<br /><br />Maybe I don't want to have to get out a magnifying glass to read a mostly textual comic. :) Maybe it's just the overpowering ennui. Who knows! But I did like Delirium's wild fantasy. :)<br /><br />Destruction probably had the most interesting idea-SF story though. :)<br /><br />Great comic! :)</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4725994141009913462">I thought that Sandman was over... how happy am I that there is more? I'm ecstatic! This is absolutely fabulous; I really need more!<br/><br/><b>
  <u>Death:</u>
</b><br/><br/>I really liked this one, but I was slightly confused at first. <br/><br/><b>
  <u>Desire:</u>
</b><br/><br/>For some reason, I have a sense of déjà vu. Was this story already featured somewhere? Because I would swear that I already read this one. <br/><b>
  <u><br/>Dream:</u>
</b><br/><br/>It's fascinating that I almost forgot that before Delirium there was Delight, at first I thought that it was a mistake. <br/><br/><b>
  <u>Despair:</u>
</b><br/><br/>Thi</span>

  <u>Desire:</u>
</b><br /><br />For some reason, I have a sense of déjà vu. Was this story already featured somewhere? Because I would swear that I already read this one. <br /><b>
  <u><br />Dream:</u>
</b><br /><br />It's fascinating that I almost forgot that before Delirium there was Delight, at first I thought that it was a mistake. <br /><br /><b>
  <u>Despair:</u>
</b><br /><br />This one was really bizarre! But I loved it. Great portrait of Despair. <br /><br /><b>
  <u>Delirium:</u>
</b><br /><br />Where the Despair bizarreness went great, this went wrong for me. Least favourite of them all.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>Destruction:</u>
</b><br /><br />I would love to see more of this. It was pretty intriguing, but it was way too short to really develop the story. <br /><br /><b>
  <u>Destiny:</u>
</b><br /><br />The story that tells all the other stories.</span>

<div id="review_2110846517" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2110846517" />
    <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin"><img alt="Bill Kerwin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442940975p2/83582.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2110846517?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=graphic-novels">graphic-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2110846517" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16042970515281451150"><br/><i>Endless Nights</i> is an enjoyable, albeit unnecessary, addendum to “The Sandman” epic: seven unrelated tales, each one of which featuring one the “Endless,” the group of seven sisters and brothers which includes the Sandman (AKA Dream, AKA Morpheus) himself. I did not find any of them particularly memorable (I had to pick up the volume again to remember exactly what they were about) but my favorites are the one about Death (which features a singularly decadent party hosted by a degenerate 18th cent</span>
  <span id="freeText16042970515281451150" style="display:none"><br /><i>Endless Nights</i> is an enjoyable, albeit unnecessary, addendum to “The Sandman” epic: seven unrelated tales, each one of which featuring one the “Endless,” the group of seven sisters and brothers which includes the Sandman (AKA Dream, AKA Morpheus) himself. I did not find any of them particularly memorable (I had to pick up the volume again to remember exactly what they were about) but my favorites are the one about Death (which features a singularly decadent party hosted by a degenerate 18th century count) and the one about Dream (which takes place during a period anterior to all the other <i>Sandman</i>, in happier days when Destruction was still an active member of the family and Delirium was still known as Delight.)<br /><br />None of these Gaiman tales are equal to the rest of the <i>Sandman</i> opus, but I found the art, if anything, superior to the earlier ten volumes. (The art of the prequel <i>Overture</i>, which came after, is even more remarkable.) I particularly liked Milo Manara’s “Desire,” whose art deceives you into thinking you are reading a conventional romance tale, until the bloody, willful conclusion disabuses you of the notion, Bill Sienkiewicz’s appropriately varied styles in the tale of “Delirium,” and—perhaps above all the others—Barron Storey and Dave McKean’s fifteen experimental portraits of “Despair.”<br /><br />As I said, this is not an essential work, and—although the stories are entertaining too—it is worth checking out for the art alone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16042970515281451150" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36890366-sud666?shelf=comics">comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36890366-sud666?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2212234047" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11623761032463106997">Endless Nights is a gem. Beautifully illustrated and wonderfully told it is yet another example of why I think so highly of Neil Gaiman. This is a collection of short stories about the Endless. Each story covers a different member of the Endless family. Each story has its own unique art style.<br/><br/>Follow the stories of Death, Desire, Dream, Despair, Delirium, Destruction and Destiny. I enjoyed the Death, Dream and Desire stories the most-though none of these stories is bad. The Despair and Delirium s</span>
  <span id="freeText11623761032463106997" style="display:none">Endless Nights is a gem. Beautifully illustrated and wonderfully told it is yet another example of why I think so highly of Neil Gaiman. This is a collection of short stories about the Endless. Each story covers a different member of the Endless family. Each story has its own unique art style.<br /><br />Follow the stories of Death, Desire, Dream, Despair, Delirium, Destruction and Destiny. I enjoyed the Death, Dream and Desire stories the most-though none of these stories is bad. The Despair and Delirium stories are rather trippy and reminded me of Grant Morrison's Arkham Asylum-style art. Some of the stories do shed some light on aspects of the Dream story (why, for example, he doesn't get along with his brother Desire). Still even those not familiar with the Sandman series will derive pleasure from these tales-this is a quality work. Blurring the lines between entertaining story and object d'art, this a is a book anyone would appreciate. I certainly did. Any Neil Gaiman fan would. Highly recommended!</span>
  <a data-text-id="11623761032463106997" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deborah Ideiosepius" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deborah Ideiosepius" href="/user/show/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius">Deborah Ideiosepius</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=comics-graphic-novels">comics-graphic-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=wordpress">wordpress</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=2020-reading-challenge">2020-reading-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer141085855" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10338413360071295702">On this, many times multiple, re-reading I still find this collection as good as ever. It may well be my favourite of the Sandman companion books (though, let's be honest; my favourite tends to be whichever I am reading at the time), partly because it is such a lush book. It has that lovely Dave McKean artwork and the textured pages and it is just a bit of an art experience to read it.<br/><br/>The stories are all quite perfect little gems in themselves, we finally get to read the story of why Dream and D</span>
  <span id="freeText10338413360071295702" style="display:none">On this, many times multiple, re-reading I still find this collection as good as ever. It may well be my favourite of the Sandman companion books (though, let's be honest; my favourite tends to be whichever I am reading at the time), partly because it is such a lush book. It has that lovely Dave McKean artwork and the textured pages and it is just a bit of an art experience to read it.<br /><br />The stories are all quite perfect little gems in themselves, we finally get to read the story of why Dream and Desire are so very much at odds in chapter 3 The Heart of a Star where we get the backstory to a few throwaway lines in The Sandman, which are key to a major story arc. I love the way Gaiman does not feel the need to over explain everything in the books. This over-explaining is a very common flaw of many otherwise good stories, both in books and film and a thing that way too many stories and franchises seem to do. Gaiman understands that not all ends need to be tided up ALL the time, but these extra stories have the internal integrity of a complete tale, with no 'fanfiction' feel to them and The Heart of a Star in particular has plenty of charm.<br /><br />Every time I re-read this, a different story is my favourite, this time it was chapter 2 Desire, What I've tasted of desire... I love the title too, one of my favourite William Blake verses. The story is a perfect addition to the Endless because in The Sandman in general, Desire is mostly posited as an adversary. But this little stand alone story gives us an isolated example of Desire's own nature and sphere of influence without any real intervention from the rest of the Endless. And it is a great story in it's own right with one excellent main character!<br /><br />As always chapter 5 Delirium, Going Inside fascinated me. It is a simple enough little story, well conceived, and while the Endless characters of Dream (Daniel) and Barnabas are familiar there is no indication of when it takes place that I can see. I do not believe it ties into any particular time frame of The Sandman. While the story is nice, it is the amazing artwork of Bill Sienkiewicz that makes this chapter sing. The crazy lines, often irregular panels and chaotic, yet amazing artwork let the right side of your brain wander along the colour and image of delirium, while the left side of your brain simultaneously follows the actual story line. At least, that is how it always feels to me while reading it.<br /><br />Anyway, perfect companion book for people who loved the Sandman, but completely accessible to anyone who didn't.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10338413360071295702" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_556089938" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/556089938" />
    <a title="Ronyell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1169488-ronyell"><img alt="Ronyell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506581787p2/1169488.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/556089938?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ronyell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ronyell" href="/user/show/1169488-ronyell">Ronyell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=modern-age-comics">modern-age-comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=neil-gaiman">neil-gaiman</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=strong-heroine">strong-heroine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=strong-hero">strong-hero</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=graphic-novels-comics">graphic-novels-comics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=my-blog-reviews">my-blog-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=bram-stoker-award">bram-stoker-award</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1169488-ronyell?shelf=locus-award">locus-award</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer556089938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16874108731165597206"><b>
  <i>4.5 stars!</i>
</b><br/><br/>After reading almost all of Neil Gaiman’s fantastic run on the “Sandman” series (to the point where I called the year 2012 “The Year of the Sandman” since I had spent the year reading the Sandman comics), I wanted to read Neil Gaiman’s latest story on the “Sandman” series, “The Sandman: Endless Nights,” even though <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25104.The_Sandman__Vol__10_The_Wake' title='The Sandman, Vol. 10 The Wake by Neil Gaiman' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Sandman, Vol. 10: The Wake</a> was actually considered the final story arc in the “Sandman” series. “The Sandman: Endless Nights,” which is also a Bram Stoker Award winn</span>
  <span id="freeText16874108731165597206" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>4.5 stars!</i>
</b><br><br>After reading almost all of Neil Gaiman’s fantastic run on the “Sandman” series (to the point where I called the year 2012 “The Year of the Sandman” since I had spent the year reading the Sandman comics), I wanted to read Neil Gaiman’s latest story on the “Sandman” series, “The Sandman: Endless Nights,” even though <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25104.The_Sandman__Vol__10_The_Wake" title="The Sandman, Vol. 10 The Wake by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sandman, Vol. 10: The Wake</a> was actually considered the final story arc in the “Sandman” series. “The Sandman: Endless Nights,” which is also a Bram Stoker Award winner and a Locus Award winner, is a fantastic series for any newcomer who wants to learn more about the Endless characters!<br><br>This volume is basically detailing seven chapters that deal with the seven siblings of the Endless and how they meet up with several unsuspecting characters in their stories. The chapters are as follow:<br><br><b>
  <u>Chapter 1: Death: Death and Venice – artwork by P. Craig Russell</u>
</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380412636i/811504.jpg" width="300" height="300" alt="Death" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><b>
  <u>Chapter 2: Desire: What I’ve Tasted of Desire – artwork by Milo Manara</u>
</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380412636i/811505._SX540_.jpg" width="300" height="300" alt="Desire" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><b>
  <u>Chapter 3: Dream: The Heart of a Star – artwork by Miguelanxo Prado</u>
</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380412636i/811506.jpg" width="300" height="400" alt="Dream" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>Chapter 4: Despair: Fifteen Portraits of Despair – artwork by Barron Storey with designs by Dave McKean<br><br><b>
  <u>Chapter 5: Delirium: Going Inside – artwork by Bill Sienkiewicz</u>
</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380412636i/811507.jpg" width="300" height="300" alt="Delirium" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><b>
  <u>Chapter 6: Destruction: On the Peninsula – artwork by Glenn Fabry</u>
</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380412636i/811508.jpg" width="300" height="300" alt="Destruction" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Chapter 7: Destiny: Endless Nights – artwork by Frank Quitely<br><br>For the past few volumes of the “Sandman” series, we were able to really get deep into the dark and mysterious world of Morpheus, better known as Dream of the Endless. Now, we finally have a volume that is dedicated to all of the siblings from the Endless, most notably Death, Dream’s older sister, Delirium, the youngest sister and Destruction, the brother who left the Endless family over four hundred years ago. Neil Gaiman has done a brilliant job at writing this volume as we actually get a closer look at not just Dream, but his siblings and I loved the way that Neil Gaiman explored each Endless character in deep detail. Probably my favorite stories in this volume were “Death and Venice,” “What I’ve Tasted of Desire,” “The Heart of a Star,” and “On the Peninsula” as these stories had the most interesting storylines and really delved into the minds of the characters being presented. Neil Gaiman’s introduction in this volume was probably the best introduction I had read out of any graphic novel (although, I do not normally read the introductions in graphic novels since I easily get bored reading them). Neil Gaiman clearly explains to the readers about why he wrote this volume after his nine year run on the “Sandman” series and it was interesting that Neil Gaiman wanted to do this volume because he wanted to work with different artists while still telling more stories about the Endless. The artwork in this volume, as with the previous volumes, are extremely well done as there are many different artists working on this volume and they include Glenn Fabry, Milo Manara, Miguelanxo Prado, Frank Quitely, P. Craig Russell, Bill Sienkiewicz, and Barron Storey. Probably my favorite artworks in this volume were done by P. Craig Russell, Milo Manara and Glenn Fabry. P. Craig Russell’s artwork in “Death and Venice” was extremely well done as the characters look slightly cartoonish, but still have a realistic touch to the characters’ appearances and I loved the way that the colors are done brightly which makes the artwork pleasing to see. Milo Manara’s artwork in “What I’ve Tasted of Desire” has a brilliant Renaissance Era vibe as the characters are realistic looking and the paintings flow well with the artwork. Glenn Fabry’s artwork in “On the Peninsula” is truly beautiful as the characters have realistic facial expressions and the environment of the peninsula is amazing to look at.<br><br>The reason why I took off half a star is because some of the stories were a bit confusing to read through. Probably the stories I had the most problems reading through were Delirium’s story “Going Inside” and Despair’s story “Fifteen Portraits of Despair” as the narratives were too complicated to understand since it seems like the stories were being told from a point of view of a crazed mind (although Delirium’s story makes so much sense based on her character). Also, for anyone who does not like violence and language, this volume does have some gory violence and strong language, although the violence is not as strong in this volume as it was in previous volumes.<br><br>Overall, “The Sandman: Endless Nights” is a fantastic book for fans of the “Sandman” series! Even though <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25104.The_Sandman__Vol__10_The_Wake" title="The Sandman, Vol. 10 The Wake by Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sandman, Vol. 10: The Wake</a> is often considered the final volume in the “Sandman” series, this volume is sometimes considered the final volume of the “Sandman” series, even though any new reader to the “Sandman” series can read this to gain a better understanding of the “Sandman” characters as a whole.<br><br><b>Review is also on: <a href="http://rabbitearsbookblog.blogspot.com/2013/03/the-sandman-endless-nights-by-neil.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Rabbit Ears Book Blog</a></b><br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="16874108731165597206" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_14611947" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14611947" />
    <a title="Rob McMonigal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/120879-rob-mcmonigal"><img alt="Rob McMonigal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487467887p2/120879.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14611947?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rob McMonigal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rob McMonigal" href="/user/show/120879-rob-mcmonigal">Rob McMonigal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14611947" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16298096940761667351">Singer-songwriter Fish has a line about returning to your past. When asked, as he often is at concerts, to play one of the seminal songs from his former band Marillion, he responds, "I'm not playing fucking Grendel!" His point is that to try and relive the past like that would make no sense--he can't sing it the way he used to, the meaning's not there. It would be nostalgic at best, off-key at worst. Trying to relive something you did years ago and have moved on from is not the best plan.<br/><br/>Which b</span>
  <span id="freeText16298096940761667351" style="display:none">Singer-songwriter Fish has a line about returning to your past. When asked, as he often is at concerts, to play one of the seminal songs from his former band Marillion, he responds, "I'm not playing fucking Grendel!" His point is that to try and relive the past like that would make no sense--he can't sing it the way he used to, the meaning's not there. It would be nostalgic at best, off-key at worst. Trying to relive something you did years ago and have moved on from is not the best plan.<br /><br />Which brings me to this graphic novel. Gaiman got his name writing an amazing series called Sandman that was already unraveling a bit by the end. He'd simply said all he wanted to do, and to make sure he was done, he destroyed his work ala the way Shakespeare destroyed his in the Tempest. He even gives us one last hurrah by linking the two together. It's a great idea. It was a great way to go out.<br /><br />But fans clamor for more. They always do. No one knows when to "close the book and gracefully decline" if I may borrow yet another musical reference. We've got Indiana Jones and the Nursing Home of Doom on deck and I just saw an ad for an all-new Rambo--still starring Stallone! If you want further proof that going back and doing more is a bad idea, I have two words for you: Phantom Menace. <br /><br />Gaiman tries his best to work some old magic in this one, but it's clear his attention is elsewhere. Death's story, brilliantly illustrated by P. Craig Russell, almost captures what used to be, but the ending twists what looked interesting into a simple "a killer justifies his life" plot line. There's some great ideas and Death as always steals the show, but it feels like it was rushed.<br /><br />Desire's story is by far the best of the collection. Folklore, myth, and tales of olden times given a modern perspective weave together as a woman uses the power of Desire to get what she wants--at a terrible cost. These are themes that feature prominently in Gaiman's prose and his current film work, and it's obvious he's very engaged in this one. The Artwork, just a bit out of focus in many ways, captures how we see something when we desire it. This was my favorite, and it would have made a great one-shot.<br /><br />Dream, the character most closely associated with Gaiman, gets rather shorted here, as his story features an ensemble cast. This is a story from the past, when Dream was more apt to love. The Endless meet up with the stars to plan the future. Dream brings his girlfriend, a young from from a planet we'll hear more about in other times. Meanwhile, Despair woos a red sun into tragic plots, and Desire does what he does best. I'm a bit surprised Gaiman tries to link the Endless to the DC universe Canon--he even mentions in the first trade of Sandman that when he tried to do that, he felt he failed. This is a story designed to show why some of the things happened the way they did in the series, and I'm not sure that was needed. It's a very clever tale, however. Yet it's not a real story about Dream.<br /><br />Despair's chapter is very rough, as expected,. As experimental graphic work goes, I'm sure it's good, but not really my taste.<br /><br />Delirium has gone a bit mad, even for her, and it's up to a few who think like she does to save her. Bill Sienkiewicz does the art duties here and his half normal art, half odd graphical work serves the story well. None of this story makes sense, but that's to be expected. It sets up the Destruction tale in the next story, where a woman goes to excavate the future--and finds some things man would be better off not knowing. Can Delirium--who's more her usual self here--and Destruction convince this archaeologist-for-hire that preservation is not always what it's cracked up to be? Again, like the Death story, I feel that this plot has been done too many times to make it worthy of what used to be written in Sandman.<br /><br />Last is Destiny, who mostly gets a character description. This is a shame, because I think there could have been a great story about how Destiny, like Gaiman, knows all, and choses that which he opts to share, just as a writer chooses what to write. This is what I mean when I said I don't think his heart was completely into the writing--none of the stories really push the boundaries the way that Gaiman does when he's in his stride.<br /><br />These are nice tales of the Endless for those who wanted more. But they're just that--more. They aren't the type of writing that makes a person who doesn't normally read "edgy" comics sit up and take notice the way that Sandman changed how I read comics. This is an encore from a man who's doing other things. Because he's so good at writing, they are still good stories. They just aren't what I've come to expect from Gaiman, and I don't think that was his fault. He felt it was time to write more Endless stories. I think he may have stretched that desire a bit too thin here. If we get more Endless, I'd prefer it to be in the form of a one-shot, so that the best idea is what we get. I'd love to read more, too--but only if it really needs to be said. (Library, 01/08)<br /><br />Trebby's Take: Not as satisfying as I'd hoped. Still worth reading, but please don't make this your introduction to Sandman.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16298096940761667351" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1720968595" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1720968595" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1452041473p2/9456990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1720968595?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/9456990-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=dark-fantasy-gothic">dark-fantasy-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=fantastic-weird">fantastic-weird</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=graphic-novels">graphic-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1720968595" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3918445250778896255"><b> Sandman, Vol 11: Endless Nights: Individual tales of the Endless</b><br/>Having just finished the 10-volume epic SANDMAN saga, it’s hard to imagine anything that can top this achievement. In aggregate, it is certainly the most ambitious comic of its time, and having depicted the character arc of Dream, also known as Morpheus and the Sandman, there is isn’t much to add to that. At the same time, since the Endless have lived for the lifetime of the current universe (and perhaps previous iterations), there</span>
  <span id="freeText3918445250778896255" style="display:none"><b> Sandman, Vol 11: Endless Nights: Individual tales of the Endless</b><br />Having just finished the 10-volume epic SANDMAN saga, it’s hard to imagine anything that can top this achievement. In aggregate, it is certainly the most ambitious comic of its time, and having depicted the character arc of Dream, also known as Morpheus and the Sandman, there is isn’t much to add to that. At the same time, since the Endless have lived for the lifetime of the current universe (and perhaps previous iterations), there are an infinite number of side-stories that Gaiman could conceive. So it was inevitable that he would choose to pen some stories that featured each of the Endless - this project itself could be endless, if there’s enough demand from Sandman fans.<br /><br />Endless Nights has a story about each of the Endless, each penned by different artists who Gaiman chose to best represent the unique aspects of each Endless sibling and their stories. As such, your impressions of the story will be greatly affected by the artists, and in the case of Despair (“Fifteen Portraits of Despair”) and Delirium (“Going Inside”), the stories are suitably grim and disorienting, respectively. <br /><br />The writing is also fragmented into poetic and cryptic snippets, so their stories are not so much stories as montages. In the case of Delirium, it makes perfect sense the the images and worlds are chaotic, disturbing, and somewhat crazed. It doesn’t make for easy reading. In the case of Despair, I just couldn’t understand what the story was about, but the artwork is suitably creepy.<br /><br />My favorite stories were about Death (“Death and Venice”), Desire (“What I’ve Tasted of Desire”), and Destruction (“On the Peninsula”). <br /><br />The story of Death is a haunting one set on an island off the coast of Venice, where a disillusioned soldier on leave recalls his brief encounter with Death as a child, and his second encounter with her as they crash the idyllic party of some decadent immortals who think themselves immune to death. The artwork by P. Craig Russell is precise, evocative, and pleasing, which is no surprise as he also did the legendary “Ramadan”. <br /><br />Desire’s tale is definitely the most sexually-charged, a fable of a young woman in early Britain who desires the handsome but playboy son of the village leader. She cuts a deal with Desire, but in typical fashion, the passions that are ignited do not conform to expectations. It reminded me a bit of the Wildlings of the North in Game of Thrones. <br /><br />The story about Destruction features my favorite, by Glen Fabry, and is a mysterious story about a remote island in the Mediterranean, where some archaeologists have unearthed some strange objects apparently from the future. The story actually features both Destruction and Delirium, and just like in previous Sandman stories, Destruction is a deeply thoughtful being, nothing like what you might expect, and is always ambivalent about his past life.<br /><br />The final story about Destiny (“Endless Nights”) is more of a brief coda or book end, and hardly counts, thought that is understandable considering that Destiny is a passive figure who knows the life of all people based on the book he carries, but he does not create or affect these life paths but merely observes. It always struck me that his role was fairly pointless, for that reason.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3918445250778896255" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1642808882" />
    <a title="leynes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45198798-leynes"><img alt="leynes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593512704p2/45198798.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1642808882?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="leynes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="leynes" href="/user/show/45198798-leynes">leynes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401242336" href="/book/show/17671912-the-sandman">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1642808882" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4988971301194924004">Okay, I really enjoyed this. I always felt that Neil Gaiman liked Death the most and so I was really happy to see that each sibling got an individual beautiful story... except for maybe Despair, but Despair is not supposed to beautiful either way.<br/><br/>Death in Venice was interesting. It was another of these mind-fucking moments of "how can times and places overlap" (are the concepts of past, present and future really just man-made and non-existent)? Of course every mail protagonist has to fall for De</span>
  <span id="freeText4988971301194924004" style="display:none">Okay, I really enjoyed this. I always felt that Neil Gaiman liked Death the most and so I was really happy to see that each sibling got an individual beautiful story... except for maybe Despair, but Despair is not supposed to beautiful either way.<br /><br />Death in Venice was interesting. It was another of these mind-fucking moments of "how can times and places overlap" (are the concepts of past, present and future really just man-made and non-existent)? Of course every mail protagonist has to fall for Death at some point but I really liked the soldier who was haunted by his childhood ghost crush. <br /><br />Desire's story was sexy. Even tho our main man was an insufferable macho. I appreciated the seduction of the woman and how she got her revenge in the end. <br /><br />Destruction's story was cool. I was really hoping for him and that scientist to hook up tho. :D I liked that he spent time with his sister and just his air in general. I would love to read more about his solo adventures.<br /><br />15 portraits of Despair was a seriously fucked-up experience. I don't know how I feel about that "story" - hell, it wasn't even a story at all. Depressing, psycho pictures and random desperate moments - fit Despair quite well. I also liked how Neil mentioned that this was actually a story about the first Despair. Neat how everything comes to a close somehow.<br /><br />Talking about coming to a close Dream's story was awesome. I loved seeing him so young and vulnerable and how Desire played her first of many tricks on him and his love life. Sucks that his lover hat to cheat on him on that big public meeting. Him being pissed off at Desire was also great. <br /><br />Delirium's story made me incredible sad. I appreciated that Neil Gaiman tried to address a very serious topic - depression, denial after a rape - and it was hard to stomach. I loved how her family and dog got her out of that shitty situation and there was hope at the end. <br />I also really digged that Delight got a cameo in (I think) Dream's story. She was soooo fun!<br /><br />Destiny's story was more of a prologue and epic. The biggest story of all in a way. <br /><br />Very neat collection, will def get a re-read in a year or so. I can't wait to read Death's solo adventures and The Ouverture story!!!</span>

<div id="review_1565795880" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_604544709" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/604544709" />
    <a title="Tanya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15698555-tanya"><img alt="Tanya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602837639p2/15698555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/604544709?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tanya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tanya" href="/user/show/15698555-tanya">Tanya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781401242336" href="/book/show/17671912-the-sandman">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer604544709" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15877888670496212587"><i>Sandman: Endless Nights</i> made history; it was the first graphic novel to ever make the <i>New York Times</i> Bestseller List. In it, each of the Endless get their own chapter, and each tale is illustrated by a different artist in an often experimental style. The stories are stand-alones and independent of each other, except Delirium's and Destruction's, which are also the only ones that take place after the events in the main <i>Sandman</i> series. I wouldn't normally review a graphic novel issue by issue, but</span>
  <span id="freeText15877888670496212587" style="display:none"><i>Sandman: Endless Nights</i> made history; it was the first graphic novel to ever make the <i>New York Times</i> Bestseller List. In it, each of the Endless get their own chapter, and each tale is illustrated by a different artist in an often experimental style. The stories are stand-alones and independent of each other, except Delirium's and Destruction's, which are also the only ones that take place after the events in the main <i>Sandman</i> series. I wouldn't normally review a graphic novel issue by issue, but since these can be considered to be illustrated short stories, and they are so stylistically different, I'll break it down.<br /><br /><i>Death And Venice</i> · ★★★★ (Art by P. Craig Russell)<br />This is the oldest story in the book, written in Venice the week after the 9/11 attacks. Its title is a nod to Thomas Mann's <i>Death In Venice</i>, and the tale itself is clearly strongly inspired by Edgar Allan Poe's <i>The Masque of the Red Death</i>. It switches back and forth between two story- and timelines: A group of people in a by-gone century, living on an island off the coast of Venice which is protected from Death by magic, and the memories and thoughts of a contemporary American soldier who met Death on that same island as a child, and never forgot the encounter.<br /><br /><i>What I've Tasted Of Desire</i> · ★★★★1/2 (Art by Milo Manara)<br />In the introduction, Gaiman says that the thought of Manara illustrating a tale of Desire was one of the things that propelled him into making <i>Endless Nights</i> a reality, and I can see why; everything from his line-work to the coloring is beautiful and sensual. This story appears to be set in pre-Roman Britain, and follows the life of a woman who bargains with Desire to win the hand of her handsome neighbor.<br /><br /><i>Dream: The Heart of a Star</i> · ★★★ (Art by Miguelanxo Prado)<br />I admit that this one tied into the wider DC universe too strongly for me to fully appreciate, so it's my least favorite in the collection—I like my <i>Sandman</i> to be self-contained. This is the earliest story in the Sandman chronology, set near the beginning of the habitability of the universe—Delight has not yet become Delirium, Death is unmerciful, and we see the genesis of the rivalry between Dream and Desire. The story is narrated by the Sun to the Earth, long before the evolution of life on it, and the illustrations involving space were by far my favorites, while I really disliked the look of Killalla of the Glow's face.<br /><br /><i>Fifteen Portraits Of Despair</i> · ★★★★★ (Art by Barron Storey, designed by Dave McKean)<br />Not really a story, but rather a collection of fifteen vignettes, or, as the title suggests, portraits, illustrating different aspects of Despair. This was some really heavy stuff, and the dark, bizarre, and often abstract art, unlike any I've seen in any other <i>Sandman</i> book (save on McKean's cover art), really enhanced the stories. This would be my pick as favorite chapter, although I had to take breaks while reading it because the sense of hopeless despair I got from these shorts was <i>a lot</i> to take.<br /><br /><i>Delirium: Going Inside</i> · ★★★★1/2 (Art by Bill Sienkiewicz)<br />I felt like I was going crazy while trying to bring coherence to these pages, until you realize that you just have to let it go, you're entering Delirium's realm after all—which tells you how well-done both story and accompanying artwork really are. It's about several mentally ill people (one of whom is loosely based on Henry Darger—I had never heard of him, but it's an incredibly fascinating story, I encourage you to look it up) who are brought together by Daniel, Dream's raven Matthew, and Delirium's dog protector Barnabas, on a quest to save Delirium from herself.<br /><br /><i>Destruction: On the Peninsula</i> · ★★★★ (Art by Glenn Fabry)<br />This directly follows and relates to Delirium's story, and features her as well. A female archaeologist who has constant dreams and visions of variations of a post-apocalyptic world is exploring an Italian peninsula which appears to hold artifacts from the future (they find pennies dated 2019—I was so glad to have found this volume now, while the future in the story is still the future!). The most sci-fi of the stories, in a way, I really enjoyed it, although Destruction's role was rather minor when compared to the other chapters so far.<br /><br /><i>Destiny: Endless Nights</i> · ★★★★ (Art by Frank Quitley)<br />Not a story, but a short vignette of the burden of responsibility that Destiny, the most elusive and mysterious of the Endless, has to bear. This chapter holds my favorite art and lettering in the volume—bright and airy full-page illustrations perfectly suited as the closing chapter in what, at the time, was the last <i>Sandman</i> volume.<br /><br />While <i>Endless Nights</i> might not be an essential addition to the <i>Sandman</i> canon, it is a delightful one, and a great way to revisit the siblings; perhaps especially because each of them gets to shine in turn. I took my time reading it and really savored it, knowing that it might be the last time I'd read a <i>Sandman</i> story for the first time. I can highly recommend it—it would be confusing to someone unfamiliar with the main series though, and is really only a treat for long-time fans, and not a good starting point for new readers.<br /><br />—————<br /><br />My other ડꪖꪀᦔꪑꪖꪀ reviews:<br /><br />01: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484243577" rel="nofollow noopener">Preludes &amp; Nocturnes</a> · ★★★½<br />02: <a The world building is fantastic because it's character based. This entire series is about each of the Endless and it's wonderful, you can't have the world without them and getting to details about them is beautiful.<br/><br/>Story: Each issue is well done and dives deep into the character that are the Endless, we get a lot of Dream in the se</span>
  <span id="freeText14609469586005577249" style="display:none">So incredibly beautiful.<br /><br />World: The art is good, it's a bit muted compared to the main series but this is still one of the more creative and different books out there. The world building is fantastic because it's character based. This entire series is about each of the Endless and it's wonderful, you can't have the world without them and getting to details about them is beautiful.<br /><br />Story: Each issue is well done and dives deep into the character that are the Endless, we get a lot of Dream in the series but here we get all the siblings and it's a wonderful read. The beautiful melancholy Death issue, the heartbreaking Despair issue, it's so good. Just read it I don't want to ruin it for anyone.<br /><br />Characters: The heart of these issues are the character and who they are and it's done so well here. We've never had a deep dive into all of them during the main series and now we do, well not super deep dives but an issue each to give readers a sense of who they are and how they are and what they are. It's good.<br /><br />A strong and beautiful character collection of issues.<br /><br />Onward to the next book!</span>

<div id="review_192509756" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/192509756" />
    <a title="Airiz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4848495-airiz"><img alt="Airiz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420337239p2/4848495.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/192509756?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Airiz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Airiz" href="/user/show/4848495-airiz">Airiz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4848495-airiz?shelf=anthology">anthology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4848495-airiz?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4848495-airiz?shelf=dark-humor">dark-humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4848495-airiz?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4848495-airiz?shelf=graphic-novel">graphic-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4848495-airiz?shelf=my-favorites">my-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4848495-airiz?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4848495-airiz?shelf=paranormal">paranormal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4848495-airiz?shelf=surreal">surreal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4848495-airiz?shelf=the-sandman-catalog">the-sandman-catalog</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer192509756" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17933123192927832958"><i>Endless Nights</i> is a sevenfold compilation of stories featuring each of the Endless, set in various times ranging from when the solar system is not yet a system in itself to the period after Lord Morpheus’ downfall. I enjoyed it for the most part, but some tales didn’t quite quench my thirst for a satisfying treat concomitant to the phenomenal ten-volume series.<br/><br/>SPOILERS ABOUND. The first installment, “Death and Venice,” is divided into two narratives: one about a kingdom in Venice whose duke made</span>
  <span id="freeText17933123192927832958" style="display:none"><i>Endless Nights</i> is a sevenfold compilation of stories featuring each of the Endless, set in various times ranging from when the solar system is not yet a system in itself to the period after Lord Morpheus’ downfall. I enjoyed it for the most part, but some tales didn’t quite quench my thirst for a satisfying treat concomitant to the phenomenal ten-volume series.<br /><br />SPOILERS ABOUND. The first installment, “Death and Venice,” is divided into two narratives: one about a kingdom in Venice whose duke made it so—with magic of some sort—that neither time nor death would touch everyone inside their gates; and one about a pessimistic soldier who comes home to Italy to visit the island where he meets a pretty gothette when he was a boy. You guessed it right—the girl is Death. The stories converge when Sergei, the soldier, meets Death again outside the gates of the said kingdom. I wouldn’t say anything anymore about what happens, but you know how it goes—when Death reaches her hand out to you, it only means you’re on your way to your final destination. This is quite a bizarre tale where our favorite demise-anthropomorph takes a backseat. I have mixed feelings about the story as a whole, but I certainly love the highlights of the soldier’s thoughts. It’s as if I can feel the emptiness he is experiencing. P.Craig Russell wows me again with his patented artistic prowess.<br /><br />“What I’ve Tasted of Desire” comes next, and from the title itself you already know which of the Endless this is about. I commend the artist because every single panel is a magnificent painting, though I have to admit I have to read this away from my parents or my sister for they might think I’m reading a pornographic comic from the olden times. Haha! Milo Manara is one heck of a good illustrator, I tell you. Anyway, the story’s about a maiden who is transformed by Desire into the ultimate seductress. When her husband is killed, she takes revenge against the murderers by using her most powerful weapon: lust.<br /><br />Next is “The Heart of a Star,” which tells yet another failed love story of our late Sandman. I sort of enjoyed this one because it explains a few things, particularly about the origins of enmity between Dream and Desire that is ever-apparent in the previous graphic novels. It as well provides a few glimpses to the earliest set of Endless when Delirium is still the cute Delight, and when the first Despair is shown, squat and ugly as the Despair we met in the series but with red tattoos all over her body (and can we say less taciturn?). The setting is in a parliament of stars where we see living suns of different planets in their anthropomorphic forms: Sol (of our very own Earth), Rao (of Superman’s Krypton), and Sto-Oa (of the Green Lantern’s Oa). I guess this is where Gaiman first applied the idea of star in human form, the same concept of his novel <i>Stardust</i>.<br /><br />What I loved the most in this compendium is “Fifteen Portraits of Despair”. There are no long storylines or foretastes of history about this Endless; instead there are fifteen vignettes about people whose hearts were snagged by the hook of Despair. Artists Barron Storey and Dave McKean joined forces to make “beautifully grotesque” (the most fitting description I could give) pieces of art that feature some characters in the micro-fictions and Despair herself. “Less is more”, they always say, and I love how Gaiman can tell the best of stories with just a few set of words. Each tale is disheartening indeed.<br /><br />“Going Inside” features Delirium. Take this as an admonition: I almost had a headache deciphering what the new characters are talking about in the first few pages. I only realized that <i>that’s</i> how everything should be, because they are actually mentally challenged people and I’ve just been inside their heads. Well, what do I say? Bravo, Gaiman…that’s one heck of a mindf**k. Here Dream--the new one--recruits five nutcases to help find Delirium, who is lost and hurt inside her own realm. Dream and the others (Barnabas and Matthew) can’t go deep inside Del’s realm because no one ever gets out with his or her sanity intact. Coupled with the marvelous art that complements the crazy tone of the story perfectly, I guess this issue achieved its main purpose. Also, I’ll just say that Matthew is as funny as ever! One line and I cracked up. No pun intended.<br /><br />The penultimate story is “Destruction on the Peninsula”. It’s about an archeologist who dreams of the end of the world, and then works at a mysterious site where artifacts from the future can be extracted. Destruction plays a minor role. In this story he is sort of baby-sitting his little sister Delirium. I’m not sure where in t <i>The Sandman</i>'s timeline this fits, but I have a wild guess it’s when Delight transfigures into Delirium for the first time. It has an interesting premise, but I’m not totally pulled in by the story.<br /><br />The last tale, “Endless Nights,” is of course about Destiny. No stories of any sort are told, but we are given a small tour to Destiny’s garden and a few information about the Cosmic Log. Very brief and interesting, I guess how this is done just fits Destiny. Whatever story we are going to be told, after all, is written on that mighty book chained to his wrist. :p<br /><br />In general, this is a quite refreshing and pleasant read.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17933123192927832958" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1125832549" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1125832549" />
    <a title="Nick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2542629-nick"><img alt="Nick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1248902207p2/2542629.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1125832549?book_show_action=true">Dec 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick" href="/user/show/2542629-nick">Nick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2542629-nick?shelf=graphic-novels">graphic-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1125832549" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5382588919400658249">Endless Nights is a mixed bag of very good and so-so in the world of the Sandman. Each chapter chronicles a tale of the mythic figures, focusing on each in the past, present and future timeline the overall series worked to establish for the better part of nine years. Up first is Death, and despite being one of the more popular characters in the series, her tale here is a bit meddling. There are two stories running parallel to one another that do end up at the same place, but Death herself isn't </span>
  <span id="freeText5382588919400658249" style="display:none">Endless Nights is a mixed bag of very good and so-so in the world of the Sandman. Each chapter chronicles a tale of the mythic figures, focusing on each in the past, present and future timeline the overall series worked to establish for the better part of nine years. Up first is Death, and despite being one of the more popular characters in the series, her tale here is a bit meddling. There are two stories running parallel to one another that do end up at the same place, but Death herself isn't present much, and what I've come to discover with her is that she is undoubtedly at her best when she's conferring with her Brother, Dream, who is absent from this story. Second is Desire, a member of the Endless who I always found underdeveloped, but really shines in her/his first standalone tale. It's arguably the strongest story in the volume. Morpheus makes his presence known in tale #3, which I also found excellent, as it worked to establish the Lord of Dreams' issues with Desire seen throughout the main series, and further developed the Sandman's place in the larger DCU (bonus points for slight nods to the Green Lantern universe). The middle chapters are where I found the most trouble, mainly with Despair and Delirium's tales. Both are experimental in structure, pacing and more clearly, art, and neither really worked for me. Despair's 15 portraits are interesting (they do not tell a story, more so brief snippets from various lives) but I found the artwork to be cloudy, confusing and rather ugly in some parts (yes, I know that is the point, but this was a bit much even to that degree). I've come to love Delirium so much that it was a big disappointment to see her scarcely used in her own tale. She's more present in Destruction's (where Gaiman finally reveals, albeit briefly, her conversion from Delight), which is a simple, yet well told story of an archaeologist searching for herself, and the danger associated with that type of wandering. Finally, Frank Quietly drew a simple concluding capsule of Destiny, which showcases his wonderful art, if only for a very brief period of time. Endless Nights served an itch that Neil Gaiman felt compelled to scratch. It is neither noteworthy nor triumphant, but will be enjoyable for those who have stuck with the Sandman this long.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5382588919400658249" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Art by Milo Manara <br/>3. Dream: The Heart Of A Star. Art by Miguelanxo Prado <br/>4. Fifteen Portraits Of Despair. Art by Barron Storey and Dave McKean <br/>5. Delirium: Going Inside.   <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer10951314139637883651">3.5 stars rounded down to 3<br/><br/>The final vignette was of Destiny. It was poetic, but ultimately underwhelming, much like the rest of this graphic novel. I adore the Sandman series, and was hoping for much more. Expectations can be terrible and all that, I guess. There <i>were</i> some excellent stories, particularly that of Death, Despair, and Delirium. As for the art, each vignette has a different artist, so it's all over the place. Most were fine, but the art for Desire's chapter was really not for me. W</span>
  <span id="freeText10951314139637883651" style="display:none">3.5 stars rounded down to 3<br /><br />The final vignette was of Destiny. <div id="review_3655115560" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3655115560" />
    <a title="GJ Refill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61290454-gj-refill"><img alt="GJ Refill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526172186p2/61290454.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3655115560?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="GJ Refill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="GJ Refill" href="/user/show/61290454-gj-refill">GJ Refill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781401200893" href="/book/show/597626.The_Sandman">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3655115560" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18299090832254007909">During the reading of Neil Gaiman’s series “The Sandman”, there were appearances from his family known as the Endless. Often, they were interesting, particularly when it came to Death and Delirium, and it was one of the things I typically enjoyed. That boded well for “Endless Nights”, a collection of seven stories. What we ended up with, though, was not very good.<br/><br/>Death. Delirium. Desire. Despair. Destiny. Destruction. Dream. These are the Endless, and apparently you have to start with a letter ‘</span>
  <span id="freeText18299090832254007909" style="display:none">During the reading of Neil Gaiman’s series “The Sandman”, there were appearances from his family known as the Endless. Often, they were interesting, particularly when it came to Death and Delirium, and it was one of the things I typically enjoyed. That boded well for “Endless Nights”, a collection of seven stories. What we ended up with, though, was not very good.<br /><br />Death. Delirium. Desire. Despair. Destiny. Destruction. Dream. These are the Endless, and apparently you have to start with a letter ‘D’ to qualify. Taking the collection of tales as a whole, there are decent parts but more than enough to not like. Taking them separately, Death is a very likeable character, and the story dedicated to her starts slowly but gets better and is ultimately satisfying. I have always like Gaiman’s ability to make her something we need not fear. Desire lives up to its name with a story featuring enough nudity to seem gratuitous while telling an acceptable enough tale. Dream is back (so I guess this narrative would count as a prequel) and is featured in a decent love story. Destruction’s and Destiny’s stories are okay but both end too quickly leaving me wanting more satisfying conclusions. <br /><br />Delirium and Despair. What can I say about these two entries? To put it simply, they were bad. Delirium is a likeable character, but the story here goes off the rails with nonsense that is difficult to follow. If you’re looking for the lower-case delirium, it’s here in full effect. Despair is the worst story, although it really should be labeled as a collection of very short tales. They are depressing (if Depression were one of the Endless, this would have qualified) and no fun. Hated this section with the harsh words and art. <br /><br />There’s stuff to like here, there’s even more to not. If you are a fan of “The Sandman”, it is worth a read but don’t expect too much. It’s not terrible, but that doesn’t make it good either. Didn’t plan this, but it happened to work out: The grade on “Endless Nights” is a D.<br /><br /><br />2.5 stars out of 5</span>
  <a data-text-id="18299090832254007909" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('LZofFc5TWSMDXON9QA/2HCA8RKqi2VIBKvSwfywHnODZx7GZGYVU/nZraiGrFqfPG5ZEF5UyceDZwJ7B07uaXw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('F+OQGPb9qWVxCztMqt/A6DGs8jBvM/97jezJVu0aUozjvj6UISukuAQ8shBBxpE7CgbyjVjY3Jp+2OfoEqZUMw==')})
  }
//]]>

Craig Russell, Shawn McManus, John Watkiss, Jill Thompson, Duncan Eagleso<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7112759893721641569\" style=\"display:none\">Fables &amp; Reflections (1993) is the sixth collection of issues in the DC Comics series, The Sandman. It was written by Neil Gaiman and illustrated by Bryan Talbot, Stan Woch, P. Craig Russell, Shawn McManus, John Watkiss, Jill Thompson, Duncan Eagleson, Kent Williams, Mark Buckingham, Vince Locke and Dick Giordano, coloured by Danny Vozzo and Lovern Kindzierski/Digital Chameleon, and lettered by Todd Klein. The introduction is written by Gene Wolfe.\n\nThe issues in the collection first appeared in 1991, 1992 and 1993. The collection first appeared in paperback and hardback in 1993.\n\nThe book contains four tales under the banner of &quot;Distant Mirrors&quot;, containing Issue #29 &quot;Thermidor&quot;, #30 &quot;August&quot;, #31 &quot;Three Septembers and a January&quot; and #50 &quot;Ramadan&quot;. Three of the issues making up the &quot;Distant Mirrors&quot; group were published between the &quot;Season of Mists&quot; and &quot;A Game of You&quot; story arcs. The last, &quot;Ramadan&quot;, was written contemporaneously, but because of art delays DC published it as Issue #50, after the &quot;Brief Lives&quot; arc.\n\nThree other issues appearing in Fables &amp; Reflections, published as the Convergence story &quot;arc&quot;, are also single-issue short stories. &quot;Convergence&quot; appeared between the &quot;A Game of You&quot; and &quot;Brief Lives&quot; story arcs.
